reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
import math


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Axial attention for 2-D feature maps.  Factorises full 2-D attention
    into sequential row-wise and column-wise attention passes, reducing
    complexity from O(N^2 * M^2) to O(N*M*(N+M)).  Used in bioimage
    analysis (cell segmentation, cryo-EM micrograph processing) and
    axial-attention protein language models.
    """

    def __init__(self, dim, num_heads, height, width, dropout=0.0):
        super().__init__()
        self.height = height
        self.width = width
        self.num_heads = num_heads
        self.head_dim = dim // num_heads
        assert dim % num_heads == 0

        self.row_norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim)
        self.row_qkv = nn.Linear(dim, 3 * dim, bias=False)
        self.row_out = nn.Linear(dim, dim)

        self.col_norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim)
        self.col_qkv = nn.Linear(dim, 3 * dim, bias=False)
        self.col_out = nn.Linear(dim, dim)

        self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout)

    def _attn(self, x, qkv_proj, out_proj, norm):
        """Standard multi-head self-attention over last-but-one dim."""
        B_outer, L, D = x.shape
        h, d = self.num_heads, self.head_dim
        x = norm(x)
        qkv = qkv_proj(x).view(B_outer, L, 3, h, d)
        q, k, v = qkv.unbind(dim=2)
        q = q.transpose(1, 2)
        k = k.transpose(1, 2)
        v = v.transpose(1, 2)
        attn = (q @ k.transpose(-1, -2)) / math.sqrt(d)
        attn = F.softmax(attn, dim=-1)
        attn = self.dropout(attn)
        out = (attn @ v).transpose(1, 2).reshape(B_outer, L, D)
        return out_proj(out)

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Args:
            x: (B, H, W, D) – 2-D feature map
        Returns:
            (B, H, W, D) – updated feature map
        """
        B, H, W, D = x.shape

        # Row-wise attention: treat each row as a sequence
        x_row = x.reshape(B * H, W, D)
        x = x + self._attn(x_row, self.row_qkv, self.row_out, self.row_norm).reshape(B, H, W, D)

        # Column-wise attention: treat each column as a sequence
        x_col = x.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).reshape(B * W, H, D)
        x = x + self._attn(x_col, self.col_qkv, self.col_out, self.col_norm).reshape(B, W, H, D).permute(0, 2, 1, 3)

        return x


dim = 64
num_heads = 4
height = 32
width = 32
batch_size = 4


def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, height, width, dim)]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [dim, num_heads, height, width]

We need to write a custom CUDA kernel to replace the forward computation of this axial attention model. The reference computes: 1. Row-wise attention: reshape (B,H,W,D) -> (B*H, W, D), apply layer norm, linear -> qkv, split, transpose, compute attention (softmax over W), dropout, then project out, add residual to x. 2. Column-wise attention: permute, reshape, same attention over H, add residual. We need to write a ModelNew class with same constructor arguments and same forward signature. We'll use torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline to compile and bind CUDA source. Given the constraints, we need to write a CUDA kernel that does the entire forward pass (or at least the heavy parts) faster than PyTorch. Since the model is small (B=4, H=W=32, D=64, heads=4), row and col attention are each (B*H, W, D) -> attention over W=32. So it's many small attention computations. The PyTorch reference will be fairly fast but potentially not fully fused: it does multiple matmuls, softmax, dropout. A fused kernel could reduce memory traffic and overhead. However, writing a fully fused multi-head attention kernel for arbitrary dimensions is tricky. But dimensions are small enough that we can have each thread block handle one (B*H) instance and W=32, D=64. Head dim d = D / h = 16. So Q, K, V are each (h, W, d) = (4, 32, 16). The attention matrix is (h, W, W) = (4, 32, 32). The whole row pass for one instance: x (W, D) -> norm -> linear (D -> 3D) -> reshape -> attention -> linear (D -> D) -> add residual. Similarly for column pass: (H, D) sequences, H=32, D=64. So for batch size B=4, H=W=32, number of instances for row pass: B*H = 128. Number of instances for col pass: B*W = 128. Thus we have 128 small attentions per pass. In a fused kernel, we can launch one thread block per instance (128 blocks), which is feasible. Because d=16 is small, we could compute attention using shared memory loads. W=32 is also small. This suggests a kernel where each block processes one sequence, threads within block compute attention for all heads or one head. So we can compute attention per head. Softmax over W dimension (dim=-1). Then dropout. Then attention @ V: (W, W) x (W, d) -> (W, d). Then reshape to (W, D). Then matmul with out_proj: (W, D) x (D, D) -> (W, D). Then add residual (x input) to output, reshape back. Similarly column pass: now sequence length L=H=32, same D=64. We can fuse the entire row pass and column pass into two kernels. Or even a single kernel that does both passes. But probably two kernels are fine, with intermediate permutations in between. However, in the reference `_attn` takes `norm`, `qkv_proj`, `out_proj`. It applies dropout to the attention weights. The reference does: x_col = x.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).reshape(B*W, H, D) Apply attention over H (length H=32). Then reshape back to (B, W, H, D) and permute to (B, H, W, D). Then x = x + result. Instead of explicit permute and reshape in PyTorch, we can have the kernel directly read x[b, h, w, d] as x[b, w, h, d] (i.e., treat w as the outer batch dimension and h as the sequence length). So we can launch blocks for each (b, w) pair. The kernel reads the input transposed on the fly. So we can have two CUDA kernels: `row_attn_forward(B, H, W, D, num_heads, x, gamma_row, beta_row, w_qkv_row, w_out_row, y)` Where x is (B, H, W, D), y is output (B, H, W, D) (in-place update or out-of-place). Then col kernel: out = out + col_attn(out). Each kernel reads its input and writes output. We can allocate one temporary buffer for row output, then pass it as input to col kernel and write final output. Or we can have the row kernel compute row_attn_out and store in a temporary buffer `tmp` of shape (B,H,W,D). Then do `x = x + tmp` in-place (or fused inside row kernel). Then col kernel reads `x`, computes `col_attn_out` into `tmp2`, then `x = x + tmp2`. Even simpler: The row kernel can write `out = x + row_attn(x)` directly. The col kernel will read `mid`, compute column attention on the fly, and write `out`. So we only need one intermediate buffer `mid` and the final `out`. Actually we can reuse: row kernel writes to `mid`. Col kernel reads `mid`, writes to `out` (which can be the final returned tensor). That's fine. Now, writing the kernel. Given that D=64, W=32 or H=32, we can use a thread block size of say 128 or 256 threads. But the work per block is: for each sequence (e.g., row), we have W*D = 32*64 = 2048 elements. We can assign each thread to process multiple elements. Let's design the row kernel. Each block corresponds to one (b, h) pair. There are B*H blocks. Block threads: we can use 128 threads (or 256). But to hide latency and get better occupancy, maybe we can process multiple (b,h) per block, or use 128 threads per block and have more blocks? 128 blocks of 128 threads = 16k threads. GPU has many SMs; 128 blocks might be enough if each block does a lot of work. Let's estimate: each block does matrix multiplications of sizes (32x64)*(64x192) and (32x64)*(64x64) plus attention. That's not huge. So occupancy might be low. But maybe it's still faster than PyTorch because PyTorch issues many kernels with overhead. Alternatively, we can have one block per head, i.e., B*H*num_heads blocks = 512 blocks. That might be better. But then we need to handle the layer norm and linear projections across threads. The linear projection (W, D)*(D, 3D) could be done by all threads in the block. But if we split by head, we need to compute qkv for that head only? Actually qkv linear outputs 3D, which is shared across heads. But we could split the qkv projection by head: each head gets a chunk of weights. However, the weight tensors are not split that way; we have full weights. We could load the full input and then each block computes a subset of output columns. But that adds complexity. Maybe simplest: one block per (b, h, head) for the attention part, but still need to do shared qkv projection. Alternatively, we can keep the qkv projection and out projection as cuBLAS-like mm using PyTorch's linear ops, but fuse the attention and residual. But the PyTorch reference already uses `nn.Linear`, which calls cuBLAS. The overhead of launching many small kernels is the bottleneck. We can fuse everything into one kernel per pass. Given the small size, a fully fused custom kernel is likely best, even if occupancy is not perfect. Let's calculate the arithmetic intensity. For row pass: - Compute mean/var for each W row: W rows * D elements. Sum and sum of squares: O(W*D) = 2k operations. - Normalization: O(W*D) = 2k. - QKV projection: (W, D)*(D, 3D) = 32*64*192 = 393k MACs. In fp32, 786k FLOPs. - Attention Q*K^T: h * (W*d*W) = 4 * (32*16*32) = 4 * 16384 = 65536 MACs = 131k FLOPs. - Softmax: h * W * W = 4 * 1024 = 4098. Negligible. - Attn * V: h * (W*W*d) = 4 * (32*32*16) = 65536 MACs = 131k FLOPs. - Out projection: (W, D)*(D, D) = 32*64*64 = 131k MACs = 262k FLOPs. - Add residual: W*D = 2k. Total per block: ~786k + 131k + 131k + 262k = ~1.3M FLOPs. With 128 blocks, total ~166M FLOPs. On a GPU that does >10 TFLOPS, this is tiny. So memory bandwidth matters. Memory traffic per block (if unfused): - Read x: W*D = 2k floats = 8KB. - Write x_norm: 8KB. - Read W_qkv: D*3D = 12k floats = 48KB. - Write qkv: W*3D = 6k floats = 24KB. - Read q, k, v: ~24KB. - Write attn: h*W*W = 4*1024 = 4096 floats = 16KB. - Read attn (for softmax and dropout): 16KB. - Write attn after dropout: 16KB. - Read attn, V: 16KB + 8KB. - Write out (attn@V): 8KB. - Read W_out: D*D = 4k floats = 16KB. - Write out_proj: 8KB. - Read x again for residual: 8KB. - Write final y: 8KB. Total if done separately: many times reading/writing small tensors. If fused: we can keep everything in shared memory/registers and only read x once, write y once. Read weights once. So total memory traffic per block: 8KB (x) + 48KB (W_qkv) + 16KB (W_out) + 8KB (y) + maybe 8KB for gamma/beta = ~88KB. That's ~11x reduction. Thus a fused kernel can be much faster, especially since the compute is small. Given that, we need to design the kernel carefully. Let's consider using shared memory for the input row, the weight matrices, and the intermediate QKV and attention outputs. But register count might be high. W = 32, D = 64. d = D / h = 16. Shared memory usage: we can store the current sequence (W, D) in shared memory: 32*64*4 = 8KB. The QKV output is (W, 3D) = 32*192*4 = 24KB. The attention matrix is (h, W, W) = 4*32*32*4 = 16KB. The output after projection is (W, D) = 8KB. Total shared memory if we keep all: 8+24+16+8 = 56KB. That's within typical 48KB per SM or 96KB/164KB on newer GPUs. But we might not need all at once. We can pipeline. But to simplify, we can keep the input and output in shared memory, and use registers for Q, K, V, and attention. Since W=32 and d=16, we could compute attention tile by tile. Alternatively, given that W=32 and h=4, we can compute each head's attention scores using a single warp or a few threads. For example, we could have 32 threads per head (one per query token), and each thread computes dot products with all key tokens. Since d=16, each thread computes 16 multiplications per key. There are W=32 keys. So per head, per query, we need 32*16 = 512 multiplications and 32*15 = 480 additions to compute the dot products. With 32 threads (one per query), each thread computes 32 dot products of length 16. That's 32 threads * 32 keys * 16 muls = 16,384 muls per head. 4 heads = 65,536 muls. Each mul is cheap. But the problem is memory: Q and K come from shared memory (or registers). If we store Q and K in shared memory of size h*W*d*2 = 4*32*16*2*4 = 16KB. That's fine. Then we can compute attention per warp: a warp of 32 threads handles one head, where thread i computes scores for query i across all keys. We can read K_j from shared memory, broadcast to warp, compute dot product with Q_i held in registers. Wait, each thread needs its own Q_i (vector of length d=16). Since d=16 and warp=32, we can store Q_i in registers (16 floats). K_j is read from shared memory (also 16 floats). So each thread computes sum(Q_i * K_j). We can loop over j=0..W-1. Since W=32, each thread does 32 iterations. In each iteration, warp loads K_j (16 floats) from shared memory. Since all threads in warp need the same K_j, it's a broadcast. Then each thread does 16 fmaf with its own Q_i registers. Then thread i writes score[i,j] to shared memory or registers. But we need all scores for softmax over j for each i. Since thread i computed score_i_j for all j, we can keep them in registers (32 floats per thread). Then we can compute softmax: subtract max (warp reduction), exp, sum (warp reduction), divide. Then we compute output = sum_j score_ij * V_j. V_j is also in shared memory. So thread i does 32 iterations, each time reading V_j (16 floats) and accumulating weighted sum into 16 registers. Then writes result to output shared memory. So per head, we need a warp of 32 threads. For 4 heads, we need 4 warps = 128 threads. That's a nice block size. Plus we might need extra threads for the linear projections and layer norm. But we can also have the same 128 threads do the linear projections. Let's think about the overall block structure. Block size: 128 threads (4 warps). We process one (b, h) row instance. The tasks: 1. Load input x[b, h, w, d] for w=0..W-1, d=0..D-1 into shared memory (SMEM) of size W*D = 2048 floats. 128 threads, each loads 16 floats. Coalesced? Input is stored in memory as (B, H, W, D). So for fixed b, h, the elements for varying w, d are contiguous in the last dimension (D). So reading D contiguous floats per w is coalesced if threads cooperate. If each thread reads a chunk of d=16 floats? Actually if thread tid reads x[w][d] where w = tid / something. Let's map threads to data. We can map thread index `tid` to a linear index over W*D = 2048. Each thread handles 2048 / 128 = 16 elements. So thread tid loads elements with linear index `tid + i*128` for i=0..15. The memory layout is (W, D) with D contiguous. So linear index corresponds to (w = idx / D, d = idx % D). Since D=64, w changes every 64. 128 threads will read 128 contiguous floats = 2 rows (since each row is 64 floats). So first warp reads w=0..1, next warp w=2..3, etc. This is coalesced. So load into SMEM: `smem_input[w][d] = x[...]`. 2. Layer norm: We need mean and variance per row w. Each row has D=64 elements. We can have warps compute per row. With 128 threads, we can assign 2 threads per element? Actually we can compute mean and variance for all rows in parallel. Since there are 32 rows and 128 threads, we can assign 4 threads per row. Each thread processes D/4 = 16 elements. We can do a warp-level reduction per row. But rows are not aligned to warps (32 threads); we have 4 threads per row. We could assign each warp to 8 rows (since 32 threads/warp, 4 threads/row => 8 rows/warp). There are 4 warps, total 32 rows. Perfect! So warp 0 handles rows w=0..7, warp 1 handles w=8..15, etc. Within a warp, thread k (0..31) belongs to row offset `row_offset = k / 4` (0..7) within the warp's 8 rows. And thread processes elements starting at `d_start = (k % 4) * 16`. So each thread reads 16 elements of its assigned row, accumulates sum and sum of squares. Then warp-level shuffle reduction to get total sum and sum of squares for each row. Then compute mean and inv_std = 1/sqrt(var + eps). Store row mean and inv_std in SMEM (32 floats each). Then all threads can read back to normalize their elements. Alternatively, we can use shared memory for partial sums. But warp shuffle is faster. Let's use warp shuffle. After computing mean and inv_std per row, each thread normalizes its 16 elements: `((x - mean) * inv_std) * gamma[d] + beta[d]`. But wait: gamma and beta are parameters of shape (D,). We have them in global memory. We can load them into registers or shared memory. Since D=64, we can load gamma and beta into SMEM of size 2*D = 128 floats. Actually we might want to keep them in SMEM. Or each thread loads the gamma/beta for its d indices directly from global memory (coalesced). Since each thread accesses distinct d, it's coalesced. So we can just load from global. 3. QKV projection: We have normalized input of shape (W, D) = (32, 64). We need to multiply by W_qkv (D, 3D) = (64, 192). So it's a small GEMM. We can compute it in shared memory. We have 128 threads. We can tile it. One approach: store normalized input in SMEM (already there). Store W_qkv in SMEM? W_qkv is 64*192 = 12,288 floats = 48KB. turn 1llm latency: 270.0s
reasoning
Let me analyze the reference model and optimize it. The model performs: 1. Row-wise attention: for each row of the HxW grid, self-attention over W elements 2. Column-wise attention: for each column of the HxW grid, self-attention over H elements The relevant dimensions are: - batch_size = 4 - height = 32 - width = 32 - dim = 64 - num_heads = 4 - head_dim = 16 For row attention: B*H = 128 sequences, each of length W=32, with dim=64 For col attention: B*W = 128 sequences, each of length H=32, with dim=64 Since H=W=32, both are symmetric in length. The operations in _attn are: 1. LayerNorm 2. Linear projection to 3*dim 3. Reshape and transpose 4. Q @ K^T (scaled by sqrt(d)) 5. Softmax 6. Dropout 7. Attention @ V 8. Transpose and reshape 9. Output linear Optimization strategy: 1. Fuse as many operations as possible into a single CUDA kernel to reduce memory bandwidth 2. For small sequence lengths (32), we can compute the entire attention in shared memory 3. We can use warp-level parallelism or a single thread block per sequence 4. Each sequence has length 32, num_heads=4, head_dim=16 Let's think about memory bandwidth vs compute. For row attention: - Input/output size: B*H*W*D = 4*32*32*64 = 262K elements ≈ 1 MB - QKV projection: 3 linear layers, each doing 64 -> 192 - Output linear: 64 -> 64 - Attention computation is relatively small due to small sequence length Given the small sizes, the kernel will likely be memory bandwidth bound, so fusion is key. Let me design a fused kernel: For each sequence (batch, row) or (batch, col): 1. Load the input into shared memory 2. Compute LayerNorm (mean, var, normalize) 3. Let me outline the row attention kernel: ```cuda __global__ void row_attn_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ norm_weight, const float* __restrict__ norm_bias, const float* __restrict__ qkv_weight, // shape (192, 64) const float* __restrict__ out_weight, // shape (64, 64) const float* __restrict__ out_bias, // shape (64,) int B, int H, int W, int D, int num_heads ) { int b = blockIdx.x / H; int h = blockIdx.x % H; // Thread id 0..127 int tid = threadIdx.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; // Layout: smem_buf1[32*64], smem_qkv[32*192], smem_buf2[32*64] float* smem_input = smem; // 32 * 64 float* smem_qkv = smem + 32 * 64; // 32 * 192 float* smem_attn = smem_qkv + 32 * 192; // 32 * 64 // Load x for this (b, h) into smem_input // x is (B, H, W, D), contiguous for fixed b,h const float* x_ptr = x + (b * H * W + h * W) * D; // Each thread loads W*D/128 = 2048/128 = 16 elements #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) { int idx = tid * 16 + i; if (idx < W * D) { smem_input[idx] = x_ptr[idx]; } } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm: each warp handles 8 rows int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; int row_start = warp_id * 8; // Actually warp 0 handles rows 0-7, warp 1 handles rows 8-15, etc. // Each row needs 4 threads. So each warp handles 8 rows (32/4=8) for (int r = 0; r < 8; r++) { int row = row_start + r; if (row >= W) continue; int thread_in_row = lane_id; // 0..31, but only 0..3, 4..7, etc. needed int group = lane_id / 4; // 0..7, one per row int pos_in_group = lane_id % 4; // 0..3 // But we already iterate rows. Let's redo: // For row = row_start + r, the 4 threads are lane_id = r*4 + p for p=0..3 // Wait, but I said each warp handles 8 rows and each row needs 4 threads. // So for row i in the warp, threads are at lane 4*i, 4*i+1, 4*i+2, 4*i+3 // Hmm, in my loop above, I said warp 0 handles rows 0-7. But actually: // row 0: threads 0,1,2,3 // row 1: threads 4,5,6,7 // ... // row 7: threads 28,29,30,31 // This is within one warp, which is good. // Wait, my row_start above is warp_id * 8, so warp 0 has row_start=0. // Then row = 0,1,2,3,4,5,6,7. // But lane_id goes 0..31. How do I map lane_id to row? // For row=r, threads are 4*r + p where p=0..3. // So for a given lane_id, group = lane_id / 4, pos = lane_id % 4. // And group (0..7) corresponds to row offset r (0..7). int r_offset = lane_id / 4; int p = lane_id % 4; int row = row_start + r_offset; // OK, so each thread in the warp works on exactly one row, and handles D/4 = 16 elements // Compute mean float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) { int d = p * 16 + i; sum += smem_input[row * D + d]; } // warp reduce sum #pragma unroll for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); } float mean = sum / D; // Compute variance float var_sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) { int d = p * 16 + i; float diff = smem_input[row * D + d] - mean; var_sum += diff * diff; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { var_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, var_sum, offset); } float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f); // Normalize #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) { int d = p * 16 + i; float val = (smem_input[row * D + d] - mean) * inv_std; val = val * norm_weight[d] + norm_bias[d]; smem_input[row * D + d] = val; } } __syncthreads(); // QKV Projection // Each thread handles 1 row and 48 columns (since 32 rows * 192 cols / 128 threads = 48) int qkv_row = tid / 4; int qkv_col_start = (tid % 4) * 48; if (qkv_row < W) { // Load the row from smem_input into registers float x_reg[64]; // too many registers? 64 registers for this... might be OK but let's use a smaller buffer // Actually, better to load on the fly for (int oc = 0; oc < 48; oc++) { int j = qkv_col_start + oc; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < D; k++) { sum += smem_input[qkv_row * D + k] * qkv_weight[j * D + k]; } smem_qkv[qkv_row * 192 + j] = sum; } } __syncthreads(); // Attention // 128 threads = 4 heads * 32 positions // tid maps to head = tid / 32, pos = tid % 32 int head = tid / 32; int pos = tid % 32; int head_dim = D / num_heads; // 16 if (head < num_heads) { // Load Q, K, V are interleaved in smem_qkv // Q: cols [head*48, head*48+16) // K: cols [head*48+16, head*48+32) // V: cols [head*48+32, head*48+48) int q_off = head * 48; int k_off = head * 48 + 16; int v_off = head * 48 + 32; // Actually smem_qkv is row-major: [pos][192] // Q for this head: smem_qkv[pos][q_off + d] for d=0..15 // Loading Q for this position float q_reg[16]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < 16; d++) { q_reg[d] = smem_qkv[pos * 192 + q_off + d]; } // Compute Q @ K^T -> 32 scores float scores[32]; float max_score = -1e20f; #pragma unroll for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < W; k_pos++) { float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < 16; d++) { dot += q_reg[d] * smem_qkv[k_pos * 192 + k_off + d]; } dot *= rsqrtf((float)head_dim); scores[k_pos] = dot; max_score = fmaxf(max_score, dot); } // Softmax float sum_exp = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < W; k_pos++) { scores[k_pos] = expf(scores[k_pos] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[k_pos]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; #pragma unroll for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < W; k_pos++) { scores[k_pos] *= inv_sum; } // Attn @ V #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < 16; d++) { float out_val = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < W; k_pos++) { out_val += scores[k_pos] * smem_qkv[k_pos * 192 + v_off + d]; } smem_attn[pos * D + head * 16 + d] = out_val; } } __syncthreads(); // Output projection // Each thread handles 1 row and 16 columns int out_row = tid / 4; int out_col_start = (tid % 4) * 16; if (out_row < W) { for (int oc = 0; oc < 16; oc++) { int j = out_col_start + oc; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < D; k++) { sum += smem_attn[out_row * D + k] * out_weight[j * D + k]; } // Add residual and bias float result = sum + out_bias[j] + x_ptr[out_row * D + j]; out[(b * H * W + h * W + out_row) * D + j] = result; } } } ``` Wait, there's an issue. In the output projection, I'm adding the residual from the original x. But the reference does `x = x + self._attn(...)`. So yes, add residual. But wait, for the col attention, the second part of the model, `x` has already been updated by the row attention. So in the full forward: 1. Row attention: out = x + row_attn(x) 2. Col attention: out = out + col_attn(out) So in our Python forward, we'd call the row kernel, then the col kernel. But in the col kernel, the residual should be added to the already-updated tensor from step 1. Now let me also double check: the output of col attention in the reference: ```python x_col = x.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).reshape(B * W, H, D) x = x + self._attn(x_col, ...).reshape(B, W, H, D).permute(0, 2, 1, 3) ``` So the output of col _attn is reshaped from (B*W, H, D) to (B, W, H, D), then permuted to (B, H, W, D), then added to x. For the col kernel, I'll need to handle this transpose in both reading and writing. Col kernel reads: for fixed b, w, read x[b, :, w, :] which is strided. Col kernel writes: write to out[b, h, w, d] = attn_result[h][d] for the residual addition. Let me plan the col kernel: ```cuda __global__ void col_attn_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ norm_weight, const float* __restrict__ norm_bias, const float* __restrict__ qkv_weight, const float* __restrict__ out_weight, const float* __restrict__ out_bias, int B, int H, int W, int D, int num_heads ) { int b = blockIdx.x / W; int w = blockIdx.x % W; // Similar structure, but loads are strided by W*D int tid = threadIdx.x; // ... shared memory same as before // Load x[b, :, w, :] into smem // x has shape (B, H, W, D) // For sequence (b, w), element h is at x + ((b * H + h) * W + w) * D const float* x_base = x + (b * H * W + w) * D; // stride between consecutive h is W*D #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) { int idx = tid * 16 + i; int h = idx / D; int d = idx % D; if (h < H) { smem_input[idx] = x_base[h * W * D + d]; } } // ... rest of attention same ... // Write back with residual // out[b, h, w, d] = x[b, h, w, d] + attn[h][d] + out_bias[d] for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) { int idx = tid * 16 + i; int h = idx / D; int d = idx % D; if (h < H) { float val = smem_attn[idx] + out_bias[d] + x_base[h * W * D + d]; out[(b * H + h) * W * D + w * D + d] = val; } } } ``` Wait, I need to be careful with indices. So loads within the same h are coalesced. But thread 4 loads h=1, d=0..15: address x_base + 2048 + 0..60. This is 2KB away from thread 0's address. For a warp of 32 threads: - Threads 0..3 load from h=0 - Threads 4..7 load from h=1 - Threads 8..11 load from h=2 - Threads 12..15 load from h=3 - ... Each group of 4 threads loads a contiguous 64-byte chunk. But different groups load from different h values, which are 2KB apart. So within a warp, there are 8 different 2KB-spaced memory segments being accessed simultaneously. This might not be fully coalesced. On H100, memory is accessed in 128-byte cache lines. So thread group 0 accesses x_base + 0..63 (one cache line), thread group 1 accesses x_base + 2048..2111 (another cache line). These are far apart but the memory controller can handle multiple cache lines per warp. Actually, with sufficient L2 bandwidth, this is usually fine for small tensors. Since PyTorch tensors are aligned to at least 256 bytes or more, and `w * D` where D=64 and w<32, `w * D * 4` can be 0, 256, 512, etc. Actually, 64*4=256. So `w * D * sizeof(float) = w * 256`. So float4 is safe. For writing back to output, similar alignment applies since output has the same shape. OK, I'll use float4 for x load and out write. Now, for the weights, can I use float4? In the QKV projection, each thread reads 48 contiguous output columns for a fixed row. Since qkv_weight is (192, 64), reading 48 columns for a fixed row means reading 48 contiguous floats from the j-th row. And the row starts at qkv_weight[j * 64]. For j=0,48,96,144, these are all multiples of 64*4=256 bytes, so aligned! For j=48: 48*64*4 = 12288, also aligned. So float4 is possible for weight reads too! But in my code, for each output element j, I loop over k=0..63. Reading from the same row j for different k values. These are contiguous, but I need them as individual floats for the dot product. I could load 16 floats at once and then use them, but that adds complexity. Since the weights are L2-cached and the main bottleneck is the shared memory for input, I'll keep it simple with scalar reads. Actually, I can load qkv_weight[j*D + k] with float4 for the inner loop over k. But then I'd need to unroll the k loop by 4. Let me see: For a fixed j, I read 16 floats at k=0,4,8,12. But the dot product requires all 64 values in order. I could load them in chunks of 4 into a temporary register array, then use them. This would reduce global memory instructions by 4x. Let me do this for the QKV and output projections: ```cuda float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < D; k += 4) { float4 w4 = *reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(qkv_weight + j * D + k); sum += smem_input[row * D + k + 0] * w4.x; sum += smem_input[row * D + k + 1] * w4.y; sum += smem_input[row * D + k + 2] * w4.z; sum += smem_input[row * D + k + 3] * w4.w; } ``` This requires D to be divisible by 4. D=64, yes. And qkv_weight[j*D + k] is float4-aligned if j*D is a multiple of 4. j*64 is always a multiple of 4. But is it aligned to 16 bytes? j*64*4 = j*256. Yes! Always a multiple of 256. So float4 alignment is guaranteed. Similarly for out_weight: j*64*4 = j*256, always aligned. And for smem_input: row*D + k = row*64 + k. row*64 is a multiple of 64. For k=0, address is row*64, which is a multiple of 64 but not necessarily 16 when converted to byte offset? row*64*4 = row*256. Yes, aligned to 256 bytes. Great! So I can use float4 for both weight and input shared memory reads in the projection loops. This is a nice optimization. Let me incorporate this. For the output projection: ```cuda for (int oc = 0; oc < 16; oc++) { int j = out_col_start + oc; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < D; k += 4) { float4 in4 = *reinterpret_cast<float4*>(&smem_attn[out_row * D + k]); float4 w4 = *reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(&out_weight[j * D + k]); sum += in4.x * w4.x + in4.y * w4.y + in4.z * w4.z + in4.w * w4.w; } // ... } ``` Wait, but the original scalar loop can be written as a single FMA chain. With the float4 unroll, we compute 4 products with a single load. This reduces memory instructions and allows more instruction-level parallelism. One caveat: for the QKV projection, the `smem_input` is accessed per thread. Since each thread handles a different row, there should be no bank conflicts when reading from smem_input (different rows, but potentially same k offset). But shared memory banks are 4 bytes each, and with float4 reads, we read 16 consecutive bytes which spans 4 banks. If multiple threads in a warp read from the same bank, there could be conflicts. For smem_input, each thread reads from row qkv_row at offsets k, k+1, k+2, k+3. With float4, it's 16 bytes. Threads in a warp have qkv_row values: tid/4. So threads 0,1,2,3 have row=0, threads 4,5,6,7 have row=1, etc. They all read from offset k at the same time. For row=0: address = 0*64 + k = k. For row=1: address = 1*64 + k = 64+k. In shared memory, bytes are mapped to banks. On H100, shared memory has 32 banks, each 4 bytes. So: - Address 0..15 maps to banks 0..3 - Address 64..79 maps to banks 0..3 again (since 64/4 = 16, 16 % 32 = 16). Wait, 64 bytes = 16 float banks? No, each float is 4 bytes = 1 bank. 64 floats = 64 banks, modulo 32 = 0. So row 1 starts at bank 0 again. So thread 0 reads banks (k/4)%32 to (k/4+3)%32 for row 0. Thread 4 reads the SAME banks for row 1. So threads 0 and 4 both access bank k/4 at the same time. This is a 2-way bank conflict, or 8-way if all 8 rows map the same. Actually, shared memory is arranged in 32 banks. Address A maps to bank (A/4) % 32. Row 0, offset k: bank = (k/4) % 32 Row 1, offset k: bank = ((64 + k)/4) % 32 = (16 + k/4) % 32 Row 2: bank = (32 + k/4) % 32 = (k/4) % 32 Row 3: (48 + k/4) % 32 Row 4: (64 + k/4) % 32 = (k/4) % 32 So rows 0, 2, 4, 6 all map to the same banks. And rows 1, 3, 5, 7 map to the same banks (offset by 16). For a warp, threads 0,1,2,3 read row 0 (banks b,b+1,b+2,b+3 for 4 consecutive floats, but with float4 it's 4 consecutive floats spanning 4 banks). Threads 4,5,6,7 read row 1 (same banks as thread 0 but offset by 16). So there might be some bank conflicts, but with 32 banks, reading float4 from different rows at the same offset should have some conflicts but not full serialization. Actually, for a float4 read from address A, it accesses banks A%32, (A+4)%32, (A+8)%32, (A+12)%32. - Thread 0 (row 0, k=0): banks 0,1,2,3 - Thread 4 (row 1, k=0): banks 16,17,18,19 These don't conflict! In general, thread with row r reads banks (r*16 + k/4 + offset) % 32 for offset=0,1,2,3. For threads in the same warp: rows 0..7 map to bank bases 0, 16, 0, 16, 0, 16, 0, 16 (since r*16 % 32). So there are 2 distinct bank bases, and each has 4 threads. So for the same k, we have 2-way bank conflicts for each bank. Actually, half the threads access one set of banks, the other half access another set. So milage may vary. But shared memory bandwidth is high enough that this is probably fine. To avoid bank conflicts entirely, I could pad the shared memory rows. For example, store 68 floats per row instead of 64. Then row 1 starts at bank (68/4)%32 = 17, row 2 at (136/4)%32 = 2, etc. But this adds complexity and memory. For 32 rows, 68*32*4 = 8.7KB vs 8KB, negligible. But I'd need to adjust all indexing. Actually, for simplicity, maybe I should just use scalar loads for shared memory and vector loads for weights. Or just accept some bank conflicts. With H100's sub-partitioned shared memory, conflicts might not be as bad. For a first pass, I'll keep it simple and optimize if needed. OK, let me actually write the code now. I'll aim for a clean, correct implementation. We can always iterate based on profiling results. Wait, one more thing. To help the compiler optimize, I should mark loops with `#pragma unroll` where the iteration count is known and small. But since D, H, W are runtime parameters, `#pragma unroll` without a number just tells the compiler to fully unroll if it can determine the trip count. But with runtime loop bounds, it might not unroll unless it can prove inlining. Actually, I can help the compiler by adding `if constexpr` but that's C++17 and works with template parameters. Since I don't want templates, I'll just rely on the compiler. For some critical small loops (like the 16 unrolls for attention head_dim), I'll add `#pragma unroll` and hope the compiler handles it. Let me write the code now. I'll write it carefully and then compile to check for errors. One more note: for `math.sqrt(d)` in the reference, I should use `1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim)` or `rsqrtf((float)head_dim)`. The reference does `/ math.sqrt(d)`. Let me use `rsqrtf` for the scale and multiply: `dot *= rsqrtf((float)head_dim)`. OK here's my plan for the code structure: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import math from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = """ #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <torch/extension.h> #include <math.h> // Row attention kernel __global__ void row_attn_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ norm_weight, const float* __restrict__ norm_bias, const float* __restrict__ qkv_weight, const float* __restrict__ out_weight, const float* __restrict__ out_bias, int B, int H, int W, int D, int num_heads ) { // ... implementation ... } // Col attention kernel __global__ void col_attn_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ norm_weight, const float* __restrict__ norm_bias, const float* __restrict__ qkv_weight, const float* __restrict__ out_weight, const float* __restrict__ out_bias, int B, int H, int W, int D, int num_heads ) { // ... implementation ... } // Host wrappers void row_attn_forward( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor out, torch::Tensor norm_weight, torch::Tensor norm_bias, torch::Tensor qkv_weight, torch::Tensor out_weight, torch::Tensor out_bias, int B, int H, int W, int D, int num_heads ) { dim3 grid(B * H); dim3 block(128); size_t smem_size = (W * D + W * 3 * D + W * D) * sizeof(float); row_attn_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), norm_weight.data_ptr<float>(), norm_bias.data_ptr<float>(), qkv_weight.data_ptr<float>(), out_weight.data_ptr<float>(), out_bias.data_ptr<float>(), B, H, W, D, num_heads ); } void col_attn_forward(...) { ... } """ # Compile inline _cuda_ext = load_inline( name="axial_attn_cuda", cpp_sources="", cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE, functions=["row_attn_forward", "col_attn_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, dim, num_heads, height, width, dropout=0.0): super().__init__() self.height = height self.width = width self.num_heads = num_heads self.head_dim = dim // num_heads assert dim % num_heads == 0 self.row_norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim) self.row_qkv = nn.Linear(dim, 3 * dim, bias=False) self.row_out = nn.Linear(dim, dim) self.col_norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim) self.col_qkv = nn.Linear(dim, 3 * dim, bias=False) self.col_out = nn.Linear(dim, dim) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout) def forward(self, x): B, H, W, D = x.shape mid = torch.empty_like(x) row_attn_forward( x, mid, self.row_norm.weight, self.row_norm.bias, self.row_qkv.weight, self.row_out.weight, self.row_out.bias, B, H, W, D, self.num_heads ) out = torch.empty_like(x) col_attn_forward( mid, out, self.col_norm.weight, self.col_norm.bias, self.col_qkv.weight, self.col_out.weight, self.col_out.bias, B, H, W, D, self.num_heads ) return out ``` This is the basic structure. Now let me write the kernel implementations carefully. For the row kernel, I'll use the 128-thread design with specific optimizations. Wait, I want to re-examine one thing. In `_attn`, the reference does: ```python qkv = qkv_proj(x).view(B_outer, L, 3, h, d) q, k, v = qkv.unbind(dim=2) q = q.transpose(1, 2) k = k.transpose(1, 2) v = v.transpose(1, 2) ``` So q, k, v have shape (B_outer, h, L, d) after transpose. Then `attn = (q @ k.transpose(-1, -2)) / math.sqrt(d)` gives (B_outer, h, L, L). `out = (attn @ v).transpose(1, 2).reshape(B_outer, L, D)` In my kernel, I need to make sure the attention computation matches this. For sequence position pos, head h: - Q[h][pos][d] = qkv_proj(x)[pos][h*48 + d] - K[h][pos][d] = qkv_proj(x)[pos][h*48 + 16 + d] - V[h][pos][d] = qkv_proj(x)[pos][h*48 + 32 + d] Then for attention: score[pos][k_pos] = sum_d Q[h][pos][d] * K[h][k_pos][d] / sqrt(d) out[h][pos][d] = sum_{k_pos} score[pos][k_pos] * V[h][k_pos][d] After attention, the output for position pos has all heads interleaved: attn_out[pos][head*16 + d] = out[h][pos][d] Then output projection: attn_out @ out_weight^T Yes, this matches my kernel design. Now, one subtle thing: the reference uses `F.softmax(attn, dim=-1)` over the key dimension. My kernel does the same. Also, dropout: `self.dropout(attn)` is applied to the attention probabilities. Since dropout=0.0, we skip it. For the residual connection: `x + self._attn(...)`. In my kernel, I add the residual when writing back. For LayerNorm: the reference uses `nn.LayerNorm(dim)` with default eps=1e-5. My kernel should do the same. Now, let me write out the full kernel code: ```cuda __global__ void row_attn_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ norm_weight, const float* __restrict__ norm_bias, const float* __restrict__ qkv_weight, const float* __restrict__ out_weight, const float* __restrict__ out_bias, int B, int H, int W, int D, int num_heads ) { int b = blockIdx.x / H; int h = blockIdx.x % H; int tid = threadIdx.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_input = smem; float* smem_qkv = smem + W * D; float* smem_attn = smem_qkv + W * 3 * D; const float* x_ptr = x + (b * H + h) * W * D; // Load x using float4 const float4* x4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(x_ptr); float4* s4_input = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(smem_input); int num_float4 = (W * D) / 4; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ((num_float4 + 127) / 128); i++) { int idx = tid + i * 128; if (idx < num_float4) { s4_input[idx] = x4[idx]; } } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; int row_group = lane_id / 4; int col_group = lane_id % 4; int row_start = warp_id * 8; for (int rg = 0; rg < 8; rg++) { int row = row_start + rg; if (row >= W) continue; // Only threads with row_group == rg participate? No, all threads participate but each handles its row // Actually, for a given warp, lane_id = rg*4 + col_group maps to row = row_start + rg // So all 32 threads in the warp naturally map to the 8 rows if (row_group == rg) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < (D / 4); i++) { int d = col_group * (D / 4) + i; // wait, D=64, 4 threads per row, each handles 16 elements // But I want col_group to handle a contiguous chunk // col_group 0: d=0..15, col_group 1: d=16..31, etc. // With D=64, each chunk is 16 elements. sum += smem_input[row * D + col_group * 16 + i]; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); } float mean = sum / D; float var_sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) { int d = col_group * 16 + i; float diff = smem_input[row * D + d] - mean; var_sum += diff * diff; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { var_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, var_sum, offset); } float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f); #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) { int d = col_group * 16 + i; float val = (smem_input[row * D + d] - mean) * inv_std; val = val * norm_weight[d] + norm_bias[d]; smem_input[row * D + d] = val; } } } __syncthreads(); // QKV projection: each thread handles 1 row and D_out/4 columns int qkv_row = tid / 4; int qkv_col_start = (tid % 4) * (3 * D / 4); // 3*D=192, /4 = 48 if (qkv_row < W) { #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < (3 * D / 4); oc++) { int j = qkv_col_start + oc; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < D; k++) { sum += smem_input[qkv_row * D + k] * qkv_weight[j * D + k]; } smem_qkv[qkv_row * 3 * D + j] = sum; } } __syncthreads(); // Attention int head = tid / W; int pos = tid % W; int head_dim = D / num_heads; if (head < num_heads && pos < W) { int q_off = head * 3 * head_dim; int k_off = head * 3 * head_dim + head_dim; int v_off = head * 3 * head_dim + 2 * head_dim; float q_reg[16]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { q_reg[d] = smem_qkv[pos * 3 * D + q_off + d]; } float scores[32]; float max_score = -1e20f; #pragma unroll for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < W; k_pos++) { float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q_reg[d] * smem_qkv[k_pos * 3 * D + k_off + d]; } dot *= rsqrtf((float)head_dim); scores[k_pos] = dot; max_score = fmaxf(max_score, dot); } float sum_exp = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < W; k_pos++) { scores[k_pos] = expf(scores[k_pos] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[k_pos]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; #pragma unroll for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < W; k_pos++) { scores[k_pos] *= inv_sum; } #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { float out_val = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < W; k_pos++) { out_val += scores[k_pos] * smem_qkv[k_pos * 3 * D + v_off + d]; } smem_attn[pos * D + head * head_dim + d] = out_val; } } __syncthreads(); // Output projection int out_row = tid / 4; int out_col_start = (tid % 4) * (D / 4); if (out_row < W) { #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < (D / 4); oc++) { int j = out_col_start + oc; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < D; k++) { sum += smem_attn[out_row * D + k] * out_weight[j * D + k]; } float result = sum + out_bias[j] + x_ptr[out_row * D + j]; out[(b * H + h) * W * D + out_row * D + j] = result; } } } ``` Wait, I see an issue with the float4 load. `num_float4 = (W * D) / 4`. With W=32, D=64, this is 512. `(num_float4 + 127) / 128` = `(512 + 127) / 128` = `639 / 128` = 4 (integer division). So each thread would do 4 iterations, loading 4 float4 each, total 16 floats, which matches. But the loop `for (int i = 0; i < 4; i++)` should be fine. Let me make sure the `if (idx < num_float4)` is correct. Yes. However, `(num_float4 + 127) / 128` in a kernel requires integer division at runtime or could be computed on host. But for our constant sizes, the compiler should optimize it. Let me just hardcode it since W*D=2048 and 2048/4=512, 512/128=4. Well, to be safe for the general case, I'll compute it but the compiler might not unroll. Actually, for the float4 loop, it doesn't matter much. The trip count is small. For the LayerNorm, I used `row_group == rg` to gate which threads compute. But this means that for `rg=0`, only threads with `row_group=0` (lane_id 0..3) participate. For `rg=1`, only lane_id 4..7 participate, etc. So in each iteration, only 4 out of 32 threads are active. This is a bit wasteful. But the warp executes all 32 threads anyway, and the inactive threads just do nothing. Is there a more efficient way? We could have each thread handle exactly one row and use all 128 threads: - 128 threads, 32 rows -> 4 threads per row - Threads 0..3 handle row 0, threads 4..7 handle row 1, ..., threads 124..127 handle row 31. - But these 4 threads might span warp boundaries. Thread 3 is in warp 0, thread 4 is also in warp 0. So all good for row 0 and 1. - Actually, threads 0-31 are all in warp 0, handling rows 0-7. - Warp reduction with shuffle works within the 4 threads of each row? - For row 0: threads 0,1,2,3. They're consecutive in the warp, so __shfl_down_sync with mask 0xF works. - But we need __shfl_down_sync to work across only those 4 threads. The standard pattern: ``` for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); } ``` This broadcasts from thread i+offset to thread i. For thread 0, it gets from thread 2. For thread 1, from thread 3. For thread 2, from thread 4 which is in a different row! This would pollute the sum. So I need to use a different reduction strategy, or use the warp-level `__shfl_down_sync` with a per-row mask. Let me compute the mask for each row: - Row 0: threads 0,1,2,3 -> mask = 0xF - Row 1: threads 4,5,6,7 -> mask = 0xF0 - etc. But `__shfl_down_sync` with offset 2: - Thread 0 wants to read from thread 2. Both in mask 0xF. OK. - Thread 4 wants to read from thread 6. Both in mask 0xF0. OK. So if we use the full warp mask 0xFFFFFFFF, the shfl still works correctly as long as we only care about the 4 threads in our row. But wait, for thread 2, shfl_down with offset 2 reads from thread 4, which is in a different row. The returned value would be from thread 4. But since we only use the result in threads 0..3, and thread 2 is in row 0, it would get the wrong value. Actually, __shfl_down_sync is a warp-level shuffle. If all threads participate, then for row 0, we want the sum of threads 0..3. Using offset=2: - Thread 0 gets from thread 2 (correct, same row) - Thread 1 gets from thread 3 (correct) - Thread 2 gets from thread 4 (WRONG! different row) - Thread 3 gets from thread 5 (WRONG!) So we can't use __shfl_down_sync directly without a mask that excludes other rows. Alternative: use `__shfl_sync` with a mask and explicit thread IDs: ```cuda float shfl_result = __shfl_sync(mask, sum, leader_tid + offset); ``` Or even simpler, just do a small shared memory reduction: ```cuda // Each thread writes its partial sum to shared memory smem_sums[tid] = sum; __syncthreads(); // For row r, threads r*4 .. r*4+3 have the partial sums // Thread r*4 (leader) sums them up if (col_group == 0) { sum = smem_sums[row * 4 + 0] + smem_sums[row * 4 + 1] + smem_sums[row * 4 + 2] + smem_sums[row * 4 + 3]; mean = sum / D; smem_mean[row] = mean; } __syncthreads(); mean = smem_mean[row]; ``` This is cleaner and avoids shuffle complexity. I'll use shared memory for the reduction. For this, I need a small extra shared memory area or reuse part of smem_input. Let me allocate a small area at the beginning or end. Actually, I can just reuse the beginning of smem_input since we're computing alpha and beta per row, and the original x values can be temporarily overwritten (wait, no, we still need them for variance calculation). Better: I'll add a small workspace at the end of my shared memory allocation. Actually, I'll just do the reduction with __shfl_sync using the correct mask. Since we know exactly which threads belong to which row, we can compute the mask. For row r in warp 0: threads r*4, r*4+1, r*4+2, r*4+3. Mask = (0xF) << (r*4). For row r in warp 1: threads 32 + r*4 .. 35 + r*4. Mask = (0xF) << (r*4 + 32). General: mask = (0xF) << (tid / 4 * 4) -- wait, this gives the start of the group. But __shfl_down_sync requires a mask of participating threads, not the source. Actually, looking at CUDA docs: `__shfl_down_sync(unsigned mask, T var, unsigned int delta, int width=warpSize);` The `mask` is the bitwise OR of the IDs of threads participating. For our group of 4, mask = __ballot_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, 1) filtered to our row. We can compute it as: ```cuda unsigned int mask = __ballot_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, row == my_row); ``` But we need `my_row` to be known. For a thread, `my_row = row_start + row_group = (tid / 32) * 8 + (tid % 32) / 4`. Then: ```cuda int my_row = (tid / 32) * 8 + (tid % 32) / 4; unsigned int mask = __ballot_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, 1); // Actually all threads participate in the sync // But shfl_down with width=4 automatically handles grouped warps ``` Wait! `__shfl_down_sync` has a 4-argument version with `width` parameter: `__shfl_down_sync(mask, var, delta, width)` If we set width=4, the warp is divided into groups of 4 threads, and shfl_down operates within each group! For width=4, thread 0 reads from thread 2 (same group), thread 4 reads from thread 6 (same group). Perfect! So we can do: ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset, 4); } ``` This is exactly what we need! The width parameter divides the 32-thread warp into 8 groups of 4 threads each. Let me use this. It's much cleaner. Now, the LayerNorm code becomes: ```cuda int my_row = warp_id * 8 + row_group; if (my_row < W) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < (D / 4); i++) { sum += smem_input[my_row * D + col_group * (D/4) + i]; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset, 4); } float mean = sum / D; float var_sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < (D / 4); i++) { int d = col_group * (D/4) + i; float diff = smem_input[my_row * D + d] - mean; var_sum += diff * diff; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { var_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, var_sum, offset, 4); } float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f); #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < (D / 4); i++) { int d = col_group * (D/4) + i; float val = (smem_input[my_row * D + d] - mean) * inv_std; val = val * norm_weight[d] + norm_bias[d]; smem_input[my_row * D + d] = val; } } ``` Wait, but D might not be divisible by 4 in the general case. blockIdx.x % H;\n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n \n extern __shared__ float smem[];\n float* smem_input = smem;\n float* smem_qkv = smem + W * D;\n float* smem_attn = smem_qkv + W * 3 * D;\n \n const float* x_ptr = x + ((b * H + h) * W) * D;\n float* out_ptr = out + ((b * H + h) * W) * D;\n \n // Load x using float4\n const float4* x4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(x_ptr);\n float4* s4_input = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(smem_input);\n int num_float4 = (W * D) / 4;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int i = 0; i < 4; i++) {\n int idx = tid + i * 128;\n if (idx < num_float4) {\n s4_input[idx] = x4[idx];\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n // LayerNorm\n int warp_id = tid / 32;\n int lane_id = tid % 32;\n int row_group = lane_id / 4;\n int col_group = lane_id % 4;\n int row_start = warp_id * 8;\n int my_row = row_start + row_group;\n \n if (my_row < W) {\n float sum = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int i = 0; i < (D / 4); i++) {\n sum += smem_input[my_row * D + col_group * (D / 4) + i];\n }\n #pragma unroll\n for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {\n sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset, 4);\n }\n float mean = sum / D;\n \n float var_sum = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int i = 0; i < (D / 4); i++) {\n int d = col_group * (D / 4) + i;\n float diff = smem_input[my_row * D + d] - mean;\n var_sum += diff * diff;\n }\n #pragma unroll\n for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {\n var_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, var_sum, offset, 4);\n }\n float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f);\n \n #pragma unroll\n for (int i = 0; i < (D / 4); i++) {\n int d = col_group * (D / 4) + i;\n float val = (smem_input[my_row * D + d] - mean) * inv_std;\n val = val * norm_weight[d] + norm_bias[d];\n smem_input[my_row * D + d] = val;\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n // QKV projection: each thread handles 1 row and (3*D/4) columns\n int qkv_row = tid / 4;\n int qkv_col_start = (tid % 4) * (3 * D / 4);\n \n if (qkv_row < W) {\n #pragma unroll\n for (int oc = 0; oc < (3 * D / 4); oc++) {\n int j = qkv_col_start + oc;\n float sum = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int k = 0; k < D; k++) {\n sum += smem_input[qkv_row * D + k] * qkv_weight[j * D + k];\n }\n smem_qkv[qkv_row * 3 * D + j] = sum;\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n // Attention\n int head = tid / W;\n int pos = tid % W;\n int head_dim = D / num_heads;\n \n if (head < num_heads) {\n int q_off = reasoning
The issue is that in `load_inline`, when `cpp_sources` is empty, the declarations go there but functions are in CUDA. I need a `cpp_sources` with declaration prototypes. Let me fix that. Also I had hardcoded 16 as head_dim in the array allocation. Need to fix this. Let me also be careful with the constraints: D=64, num_heads=4, so head_dim=16, but I shouldn't use a fixed size array unless I'm sure. Let me make it dynamic. Also I have a bug: `float q_reg[16]` hardcoded array but head_dim might vary. Let me fix this properly and provide a `cpp_sources` string with forward declarations.

The QKV projection loop doesn't cover all of 3*D outputs - with 128 threads, tid/4 = 32 rows but we only have W=32 rows, and each thread does 3*D/4 = 48 columns... but with only 6 sum variables, we only compute 48 columns per thread? Same issue with output projection - only 4 sums for D/4 = 16 columns. 3. QKV weight access pattern is probably wrong - Linear.weight is (out_features, in_features) so it should be qkv_weight[j, k] but CUDA arrays are flat. 4. Attention: there's also an issue with how QKV is stored in shared memory. Let me write a much cleaner, simpler kernel that I know is correct, then optimize from there. For this problem, D = 64, H = W = 32, B = 4, num_heads = 4, head_dim = 16. Each row/col has 32 elements of 64 dim. The attention is small enough that we can use shared memory heavily, but we need correctness first. Let me write a simpler kernel where each thread block handles one row (for row attention) or one column (for col attention), and we have enough threads to do the work directly. For row attention: W = 32, D = 64. A block of 128 threads can handle 1 row. - 32 threads for LayerNorm reductions (one warp, all dims, very fast). - Each of 128 threads can compute part of the QKV projection. - Each of 128 threads can compute attention for fractional heads. - Wait: num_heads * W = 4 * 32 = 128 total (head, position) pairs. So each thread can handle exactly one pair! That's elegant! Each thread block handles one row, and each thread handles one attention head-position pair. For QKV projection: W * 3D = 32 * 192 = 6144 output elements. With 128 threads, each thread does 48 elements. For output projection: W * D = 32 * 64 = 2048 elements. With 128 threads, each thread does 16 elements. For LayerNorm: 32 rows of 64 elements each. Actually, the simplest fix is to use loops instead of hardcoded sums. And the attention: each thread computing one (head, pos) works nicely with 128 threads = 4 heads * 32 positions. Let me rewrite more carefully. I'll also use const expression for head_dim. Actually, wait, let me reconsider whether unbind and the memory layout is correct. In PyTorch: - qkv_proj(x) produces (B, L, 3*D) - .view(B, L, 3, h, d) gives layout: (B, L, 3, h, d) - q, k, v = qkv.unbind(dim=2) giving each of shape (B, L, h, d) - .transpose(1, 2) gives (B, h, L, d) - attn = q @ k.transpose(-1, -2) gives (B, h, L, L) - softmax over dim=-1 - attn @ v gives (B, h, L, d) - .transpose(1, 2).reshape(B, L, D) So for position pos under head head: q = qkv[head, 0, pos, 0:head_dim] k = qkv[head, 1, pos, 0:head_dim] -- wait, after unbind(dim=2): q has shape (B, L, h, d), so q[b, pos, head] is the query for that head and position. Similarly k[b, k_pos, head]. So in q @ k^T, we compute for all pairs (pos, k_pos) under each head. q[b, :, head] @ k[b, :, head]^T So q[b, pos, head] should dot with k[b, k_pos, head] for all k_pos. My layout in shared memory: smem_qkv[pos * 3 * D + q_off + d] q_off = head * 3 * head_dim k_off = head * 3 * head_dim + head_dim Wait, the original view was (B, L, 3, h, d). So for a given pos: qkv[pos, 0, head, d] = query qkv[pos, 1, head, d] = key qkv[pos, 2, head, d] = value If I store as smem_qkv[pos * 3 * D + 0 * (h*d) + head * d + d_local], that's: pos * 3D + head * head_dim + d_local for query pos * 3D + h*d + head * head_dim + d_local for key pos * 3D + 2*h*d + head * head_dim + d_local for value where h*d = D. So: q_off = head * head_dim k_off = D + head * head_dim v_off = 2 * D + head * head_dim But in my code I had: q_off = head * 3 * head_dim k_off = head * 3 * head_dim + head_dim v_off = head * 3 * head_dim + 2 * head_dim Which is completely wrong! The interleaving is wrong. Correct layout: For each position pos, the 3*D vector is [q_all_heads, k_all_heads, v_all_heads] where q_all_heads = [head0_q, head1_q, ..., head_h_q], each head_q is head_dim elements. So: q for (head, d) is at: pos * 3D + head * head_dim + d k for (head, d) is at: pos * 3D + D + head * head_dim + d v for (head, d) is at: pos * 3D + 2D + head * head_dim + d This is a major bug. Also, in the QKV projection, the weight layout: nn.Linear(weight) is (out_features, in_features). The PyTorch linear computes x @ W^T, or equivalently W^T @ x. In CUDA, if I access qkv_weight[j * D + k], I'm assuming row-major where each output dim j has all input dims k. This matches (out_features, in_features) layout when accessed as [j, k]. qkv_proj(x)[..., j] = sum_k x[..., k] * W[j, k] So qkv_weight[j * D + k] is correct since out_features = 3*D, in_features = D. Similarly for output projection. But in the QKV computation, I was computing: smem_qkv[qkv_row * 3 * D + j] = sum where j goes from qkv_col_start to qkv_col_start + num_col. And fix the attention offset bug. Also, for the col_attn load: x_base is the first element of column w for batch b. But x_base + h * W * D + d doesn't point to the right element. The layout is [b, H, W, D]. For column w, the H elements are at: x[b, 0, w, d] = base + (0 * W + w) * D + d x[b, 1, w, d] = base + (1 * W + w) * D + d x[b, h, w, d] = base + (h * W + w) * D + d So x_base was set to inp + (b * H * W + w) * D which is wrong! b * H * W + w is batch_offset + w, which points to x[b, 0, w, 0] only if W=1. Correct: x_base = inp + (b * H * W + w) * D Wait, that gives x[b, 0, w, 0]... Let's check: idx = ((b * H + h) * W + w) * D + d For h=0, w=0: idx = (b * H * W + 0) * D For h=0, w=1: idx = (b * H * W + 1) * D So x[b, 0, w, :] starts at (b*H*W + w) * D x[b, 1, w, :] starts at (b*H*W + W + w) * D = (b*H*W + w) * D + W*D x[b, h, w, :] starts at (b*H*W + w) * D + h*W*D So x_base = inp + ((b * H * W) + w) * D is correct for h=0. And x_base + h * W * D gives x[b, h, w, 0]. Wait but (b * H * W + w) * D = b * H * W * D + w * D. For h=0: offset = w * D, element x[b, 0, w, d] is at b*H*W*D + w*D + d For h=1: offset = W*D + w*D, element x[b, 1, w, d] is at b*H*W*D + W*D + w*D + d Yes, x_base + h * W * D + d is correct! So the load looks correct. But the float4 load computes: h = (idx * 4) / D, d = (idx * 4) % D If idx corresponds to SMEM element number (in groups of 4 floats), then idx*4 is the float offset in smem. Since we have H*D floats in smem_input, this maps linearly. The float4 load from HBM: x4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(x_base + h * W * D + d) But for h=0, d goes from 0 to D-1. That's x_base[0..D-1], correct. For h=1, d goes from 0 to D-1. That's x_base[W*D .. W*D+D-1] which is x[b,1,w,0..D-1], correct. So the column load is correct. Now let me fix all the bugs. reasoning
The variable name is wrong - I used `smem_qkv` when I named it `s_qkv`. Let me fix this with a much cleaner, simpler implementation. I'll focus on correctness first, then optimize. Let me think about where the previous kernels had correctness issues: 1. The QKV weight layout - nn.Linear stores weights as (out_features, in_features), so when we access qkv_weight[j * D + k], we need to make sure this is correct for (3*D, D) shape. Let me double-check: `self.row_qkv.weight` shape is (3*D, D). The element for output j and input k is at index j * D + k in row-major. Yes, the linear layer weights are transposed when stored internally, but at the CUDA level they're already in the correct contiguous order. Wait, actually `nn.Linear` stores weight as (out_features, in_features) which is (3*D, D). But internally, for matrix multiplication, the access pattern depends on how they're stored. Let me check: the weight is accessed as weight[out, in] by the CUDA kernel. But in PyTorch linear, the matmul is x @ W^T, where W is stored as (out, in). So in the kernel, to match x @ W^T, we'd access W^T[k, j] = W[j, k]. If the weight is stored as (out, in) = (3D, D), then the flat index is j * D + k for output j, input k. The operation `torch.matmul(x, W.T)` means element for output j is sum_k x[k] * W[j, k], so yes we access W[j * D + k]. Wait, but I was getting large errors before. Let me think about what might be wrong. Actually, let me just write a much more careful implementation. Actually, one issue with the first kernel was the QKV projection: I used `(tid % 4) * (3 * D / 4)` which is `0, 48, 96, 144` for D=64 (3D=192, 3D/4=48). But I only unrolled 6 elements when each group should have 48 columns. The conditionals `if (3*D >= qkv_col_start + N)` were true for all, but I wasn't covering all 48 columns per thread, only 6. That would explain the huge error. Let me write a clean, correct version now, using `for` loops with correct bounds and focusing on getting the math right.

In PyTorch default LayerNorm, eps=1e-5. Good. Potential issue: warp divergence with `if (row < W)` in a warp. But since all threads within a warp either access the same or diverge within block. Since W=32, each block for row processes 32 rows, and with 128 threads, each warp handles 8 rows. So 4 warps handle 32 rows: first 4 warps handle 8 each? Wait: - warp 0 (lanes 0-31): row = 0*8 + lane/4. For lane 0..31, row = lane/4, so rows 0,0,0,0,1,1,1,1,...7,7,7,7. That's 8 rows with 4 threads each. - warp 1: rows 8..15 - warp 2: rows 16..23 - warp 3: rows 24..31 That's fine. But what about warps 4 and beyond? If we have 128 threads = 4 warps? Wait, 128/32 = 4 warps exactly. So warp IDs 0,1,2,3. All threads have row < W because warp 3 gives rows 24..31. So actually all 128 threads participate! Great, no divergence for row layer norm. Similarly for column attention: H=32, 4 warps handle rows 0..31. All participate. But there is a potential issue: the attention section: `int head = tid / W` for row (W=32, num_heads=4). tid 0-127: head 0 for tid 0-31, head 1 for 32-63, head 2 for 64-95, head 3 for 96-127. That's exactly 4*32=128 = all threads. No divergence. For column: `int head = tid / H` similar. So all threads in a warp do the same instruction, which is great. But for QKV and out projections, the `for (int i = tid; i < qkv_total; i += blockDim.x)` loop: each thread does series of iterations. With QKV total = 32 * 192 = 6144, blockDim.x=128, each thread does 48 iterations. For those iterations, all threads in a warp participate (48 = warp continuous), good. For out projection: W*D = 32*64 = 2048, blockDim=128, each thread 16 iterations. 16 is 1/2 warp. All threads in warp participate. Good. One concern: the `for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++)` loops inside attention: for head_dim=16, each thread has 16 iterations. This loop is inside `if (head < num_heads)` which is all threads, so no warp divergence. Another concern: the softexp does `scores[kpos] = expf(scores[kpos] - max_sc);` which is fine. In the reference: ```python qkv = qkv_proj(x).view(B_outer, L, 3, h, d) q, k, v = qkv.unbind(dim=2) q = q.transpose(1, 2) ``` After unbind, q has shape (B_outer, L, h, d) and q.transpose(1,2) has shape (B_outer, h, L, d). In PyTorch, `.view(B_outer, L, 3, h, d)` only works if the tensor is contiguous in the right way. Since qkv_proj returns a contiguous tensor of shape (B_outer, L, 3*h*d), and we view it as (B_outer, L, 3, h, d), the memory layout is [B_outer, L, 3, h, d]. Then unbind on dim=2 gives q with shape (B_outer, L, h, d). The memory layout of q is: for each batch and position, the h*d values are contiguous. Specifically: q[b, l, h, d] is at index b*L*h*d + l*h*d + h*d + d_idx Wait, the h dimension varies fastest after l, and d is inside h. So for fixed b and l, the data is [head0_d0, head0_d1, ..., head0_d15, head1_d0, ..., head3_d15]. Then q.transpose(1,2) gives shape (B_outer, h, L, d). Since we're swapping dimensions 1 and 2, the stride for the new dim 1 (heads) is h*d (the old distance between consecutive Ls), and the stride for new dim 2 (L) is d (the old distance between consecutive heads). So q[b, h, l, d] is at: b * (L*h*d) + h * (L*d) wait no. Original strides of q after unbind: [L*h*d, h*d, d, 1] After transpose(1,2): the new strides are: - dim 0: L*h*d - dim 1: d (old stride of dim 2, which is d) - dim 2: h*d (old stride of dim 1, which is h*d) - dim 3: 1 Wait, let me verify. Original shape (B, L, h, d), strides [L*h*d, h*d, d, 1]. Transpose dims 1 and 2: new shape (B, h, L, d). New strides: - dim 0: L*h*d (unchanged) - dim 1 (was dim 2): d - dim 2 (was dim 1): h*d - dim 3: 1 So index = b * L*h*d + h * d + l * h*d + d_idx. OK this is getting complicated. What if I just keep the tensors contiguous by not transposing in Python, but instead handle the transpose inside the kernel? If I pass q as (B*H, W, num_heads, head_dim) without transpose, then inside the kernel I need to compute attention with q[b, w, h, d] and k[b, kpos, h, d]. The dot product for attention scores should be along the d dimension for fixed h. But in the reference, after transpose, the batched matmul treats all dimensions except the last two as batch. So in the transposed layout, q[b, h, w, :] @ k[b, h, :, w] gives attention. In the non-transposed layout, q[b, w, h, :] and k[b, kpos, h, :] have the head dim at position 2. We still want `sum_d q[b, w, h, d] * k[b, kpos, h, d]`. So inside the kernel, for each batch b, head h, position w, and key position kpos: score = sum_d q[b, w, h, d] * k[b, kpos, h, d] And the output should be: out[b, w, h, d] = sum_{kpos} softmax(score)[w, kpos] * v[b, kpos, h, d] Then after the kernel, I reshape to (B*H, W, D) and pass to out_proj. This approach keeps q, k, v in their original contiguous layout after unbind, avoiding the transpose overhead! Let me define the custom kernel to accept q, k, v in layout (B_outer, L, num_heads, head_dim). For row attention, B_outer = B*H = 128, L=W=32. For column attention, B_outer = B*W = 128, L=H=32. So total elements to process: 128 * 32 * 4 * 16 = 262,144. For the attention kernel, each block can process one batch item (b), and each thread can process one (head, pos) pair. With 128 threads per block and num_heads*L = 4*32 = 128, each block has exactly the right number of threads! For each batch b: - Load all q, k, v for that batch into shared memory? That's L * num_heads * head_dim * 3 = 32 * 4 * 16 * 3 = 6144 floats per batch. That's small. - Actually, each block handles one batch, so shared memory per block = L*D*3 = 32*64*3 = 6144 floats = 24KB. Very manageable. - With 128 threads, we can load everything easily. Wait, but the Q, K, V layout is (B_outer, L, num_heads, head_dim). To load into shared memory in a convenient layout, we might want to rearrange. But since num_heads*head_dim = D, we can just load them as (L, D) matrices. For batch b: - q_ptr = q.data_ptr<float>() + b * L * D - k_ptr = k.data_ptr<float>() + b * L * D - v_ptr = v.data_ptr<float>() + b * L * D Since q has shape (B_outer, L, num_heads, head_dim) and num_heads*head_dim = D, the total size per L is D. So we can treat it as (B_outer, L, D). Then the attention computation is the same as before: for each head and position, compute dot product with all key positions. Wait, but for attention scores, we need: score[pos, kpos, head] = sum_d q[pos, head, d] * k[kpos, head, d] In the (L, D) view, q[pos, :] is contiguous, but within that, the head segments are contiguous. This is the same layout as my previous s_qkv array, just split into three separate tensors. So I can reuse my previous attention logic with separate Q, K, V shared memory arrays. And for output: out[pos, head, d] = sum_kpos softmax(score)[pos, kpos] * v[kpos, head, d] This is also the same. So the custom attention-only kernel is very similar to what I already wrote, just without the QKV and output projection parts. This should DEFINITELY be numerically correct because Q, K, V come from PyTorch's matmul exactly as in the reference, and the attention formula is exactly the same. Let me code this up. In the reference: ```python attn = (q @ k.transpose(-1, -2)) / math.sqrt(d) ``` This is a batched matrix multiply. For head_dim=16, it's a small GEMM. For W=32, it's 32x16 @ 16x32 = 32x32. In my kernel, I do the dot product manually. For 16 elements, the rounding error from different summation order is minuscule. Let me check if PyTorch's softmax matches mine. But I need to make sure the output layout matches what PyTorch expects. In the reference: ```python out = (attn @ v).transpose(1, 2).reshape(B_outer, L, D) ``` After `attn @ v`, the result has shape (B_outer, h, L, d). Then `transpose(1, 2)` gives (B_outer, L, h, d). Then `reshape(B_outer, L, D)` flattens h*d into D. In my kernel, I write output as `s_out[pos * D + head * head_dim + d]`. So the kernel outputs a tensor of shape (B_outer, L, D) directly. Here's the hybrid kernel design: ```cpp __global__ void attn_from_qkv_kernel( const float* __restrict__ qkv, float* __restrict__ out, int B_outer, int L, int D, int num_heads ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int head_dim = D / num_heads; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* s_qkv = smem; // L * 3 * D float* s_out = smem + L * 3 * D; // L * D const float* qkv_ptr = qkv + b * L * 3 * D; for (int i = tid; i < L * 3 * D; i += blockDim.x) s_qkv[i] = qkv_ptr[i]; __syncthreads(); int head = tid / L; int pos = tid % L; if (head < num_heads) { float q[16]; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) q[d] = s_qkv[pos * 3 * D + head * head_dim + d]; float scores[32]; float max_sc = -1e30f; for (int kpos = 0; kpos < L; kpos++) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) dot += q[d] * s_qkv[kpos * 3 * D + D + head * head_dim + d]; dot *= rsqrtf((float)head_dim); scores[kpos] = dot; max_sc = fmaxf(max_sc, dot); } float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int kpos = 0; kpos < L; kpos++) { scores[kpos] = expf(scores[kpos] - max_sc); sum_exp += scores[kpos]; } float isum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { float acc = 0.0f; for (int kpos = 0; kpos < L; kpos++) acc += scores[kpos] * s_qkv[kpos * 3 * D + 2 * D + head * head_dim + d]; s_out[pos * D + head * head_dim + d] = acc * isum; } } __syncthreads(); float* out_ptr = out + b * L * D; for (int i = tid; i < L * D; i += blockDim.x) out_ptr[i] = s_out[i]; } ``` This kernel is simple, clean, and should produce numerically equivalent results to PyTorch's attention because: 1. QKV values are from PyTorch 2. Attention formula is identical 3. The output layout matches Then in Python: ```python def forward(self, x): B, H, W, D = x.shape # Row x_row = x.reshape(B*H, W, D) x_norm = self.row_norm(x_row) qkv = self.row_qkv(x_norm) attn_out = torch.empty(B*H, W, D, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) _cuda_ext.attn_from_qkv(qkv, attn_out, B*H, W, D, self.num_heads) x = x + self.row_out(attn_out).reshape(B, H, W, D) # Col x_col = x.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).reshape(B*W, H, D) x_norm = self.col_norm(x_col) qkv = self.col_qkv(x_norm) attn_out = torch.empty(B*W, H, D, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) _cuda_ext.attn_from_qkv(qkv, attn_out, B*W, H, D, self.num_heads) x = x + self.col_out(attn_out).reshape(B, W, H, D).permute(0, 2, 1, 3) return x ``` This should be correct. Performance might not be as good as fully fused, but it avoids creating the large attn intermediate tensor and fuses score+softmax+value into one kernel. Plus we avoid the transpose ops in Python for q, k, v. Wait, in the reference, qkv is viewed as (B_outer, L, 3, num_heads, head_dim) and then unbind + transpose. My kernel directly reads from the (B_outer, L, 3*D) layout and computes attention output in (B_outer, L, D) layout. This avoids all intermediate tensor creations for q, k, v, and attn. The only remaining intermediate is `attn_out` of shape (B_outer, L, D), which is needed anyway for the output projection. Number of kernel launches per attention: 1. row_norm (PyTorch) 2. row_qkv (PyTorch GEMM) 3. custom_attn (my kernel) 4. row_out (PyTorch GEMM) Plus reshape/add overhead which is minimal. The reference does: 1. row_norm (kernel) 2. row_qkv (GEMM) 3. reshape + unbind + transpose (views) 4. q @ k.T (GEMM) 5. softmax (kernel) 6. dropout (identity) 7. attn @ v (GEMM) 8. transpose + reshape (views) 9. row_out (GEMM) 10. add + reshape (kernel) So my approach saves: 1 GEMM (q@k), 1 kernel (softmax), 1 GEMM (attn@v), and all the view/transpose overhead. Plus I avoid the 2MB intermediate attn tensor. The downside is that my custom attention kernel might not be as optimized as cuBLAS GEMM for the two small matmuls. But on H100, a custom kernel that loads data into shared memory once and reuses it for both matmuls should be competitive, especially since the matrices are tiny (32x16 and 32x32). Let me implement this hybrid kernel. Actually, I realize I should also handle the dropout. But the reference has `dropout=0.0` by default, so dropout is identity. I can ignore it. And one more thing: in the reference, there are `view` and `transpose` operations which in PyTorch don't copy memory but change strides. My approach with the custom kernel completely eliminates these by treating the data in its original memory layout. Let me write the code now.
